Abstract
The thesis mainly aims to study two aeroelastic wind turbine design software-ASHES and HAWC2
in details and also to compare the analysis results obtained from a wide range of simulations on
code-to code and code-to-experiments level.
       For comparison of code-to-code benchmarking, two wind turbine models: DTU 10MW
Reference Wind Turbine and NREL 5-MW Baseline Wind Turbine are chosen and simulations are
performed for stiff and flexible rotor respectively. Every single simulation lasts for 450 seconds, but
only last 300 seconds data are taken into consideration to avoid transient state. Simulations are
performed for 22 different wind speeds (4 m/s to 25 m/s). Mechanical power, thrust, power
coefficient and thrust coefficient are thereby plotted to show the variation between ASHES and
HAWC2. Relative mismatch of different basic properties is also calculated.
       NTNU Blind Test (NTNU Stiff Rotor) is chosen for comparison of code-to-experiments
benchmarking. Simulations are performed for variable tip speed ratio (1 to 11.3) by keeping both
the wind speed and the pitch angle constant as 10 m/s and 0 degree respectively. The comparison is
mainly based on two properties: power coefficient and thrust coefficient. Relative error or mismatch
between properties is also carried out. Unfortunately, NTNU Blind Test (NTNU Stiff Rotor) is only
compared with ASHES. Lack of structural data input has made it impossible to perform the
simulation in HAWC2.
       Moreover, span wise distribution of various properties like angle of attack, lift coefficient, drag
coefficient, axial induction factor, tangential induction factor, thrust loading and tangential loading
are also plotted and discussed for in depth comparison of code-to-code benchmarking. The anomaly
found in span wise distribution of lift coefficient and thrust loading is briefly explained. The type of
bugs that need to be corrected in the future version of ASHES and HAWC2 are also identified.

==PAGE:14==
Chapter 1
Introduction
1.1 Background
The reserves of conventional energy resources like oil, coal and natural gas are declining day by
day. As a result, the world is experiencing a huge economic crisis in terms of energy balance and
distribution. One way to solve this problem is to reduce the energy consumption rate. Nowadays it
is quite impossible to do so due to the fact that no innovation can be done without energy
consumption. Hence, it is imperative to search for new energy resources other than conventional
ones. Here lies the importance of renewable energy resources- not only to fulfill the current energy
demand but also to safeguard the future of human life due to its green, clean nature.
           The ‘Europe 2020’ strategy (a strategy that aims at 20% reduction of greenhouse gases as
well as 20% increments of renewable energy share in total energy consumption within the year
2020) is no longer a dream rather a reality. The wind energy industry especially the offshore sector
is getting popular day by day across the Europe due to the enormous wind energy potential of the
North Sea. United Kingdom, Germany, Denmark are quite keen to use this huge potential and
thereby increasing their total share of renewable energy production and consumption. The day is not
far when not only Europe but also throughout the world, offshore wind energy industry will become
an important sector in the energy market. In this regard, the importance of reliable aeroelastic wind
turbine design software knows no bounds.  
           This thesis is an attempt to visualize the real wind turbine scenario through the comparison
of code-to-code and code-to-experiments benchmarking. In order to do so, two aeroelastic wind
turbine design software: ASHES and HAWC2 are used throughout the thesis. At first, only the basic
properties like mechanical power, thrust, power coefficient and thrust coefficient are taken into
consideration. Next, span wise distribution of angle of attack, lift coefficient, drag coefficient, axial
induction factor, tangential induction factor, thrust loading and tangential loading for different wind
speeds are plotted and discussed. The relative comparison of different properties between ASHES
and HAWC2 is one of the interesting features of this thesis. Moreover, the potential sources of bugs
and their improvements are figured out in a simple manner. Last but not the least, the future work of
the thesis is suggested for the interested ones who are keen to implement innovative ideas regarding
12
==PAGE:15==
the comparison of code-to-code and code-to-experiments benchmarking.
1.2   Thesis outline
  
The thesis is structured as follows:
          Chapter 2 illustrates related theory including simple understanding of ASHES and HAWC2,
the definition of their input files, Blade Element Momentum (BEM) method, Glauert empirical
relation, Wilson and Walker correction, basic definition of wind turbine etc.
           Chapter 3 consists of all details regarding case 1-DTU 10MW Reference Wind Turbine (Stiff
Rotor) and its description, aerodynamic design layout, simulation setup, result analysis, comparison
of code-to-code benchmarking etc.  
         Chapter 4 is all about case 2-NREL 5-MW Baseline Wind Turbine (Flexible Rotor) and its
detailed description, aerodynamic design layout, simulation setup, result analysis, comparison of
code-to-code benchmarking etc.
        Chapter 5 represents an overview of case 3-NTNU Blind Test (NTNU Stiff Rotor) and its
description, aerodynamic design layout, simulation setup, result analysis, comparison of code-to-
experiments benchmarking etc.
         Chapter 6 highlights the main contribution of the thesis including detailed observation and
challenges, sources of bugs present in ASHES and HAWC2, proposed solution for the bugs,
important features to be included in the future etc.  
        Chapter 7 draws conclusion of the thesis outlining the limitations and drawbacks. It also
suggests about the possible scope of future work.
            References document all the scientific resources used throughout the thesis.
      Appendix includes input files and post processing files used for ASHES and HAWC2
throughout the thesis.
13
==PAGE:16==
Chapter 2
Theory    
2.1   ASHES  
ASHES is an aeroelastic software basically used for designing purpose of wind turbines. The
software has been developed by Simis AS. Its main function is to perform wind turbine simulations
as  accurately  as  possible  and  also  to  determine  their  behavior  when  subjected  to  different
aerodynamic and hydrodynamic loads.  [1]
         The basic criterion that makes it special from other wind turbine design software is that
ASHES has a user friendly graphical interface (see Figure 1.1). [2]
Figure 2.1 Graphical user interface of ASHES [2]
2.1.1   Features of ASHES
ASHES also provides some interesting features such as an intuitive, modern and user friendly
14
==PAGE:17==
interface, real time 3-D visualization, model templates, advanced integrated analyses and efficient
parallel jobs. [2]
         The main purpose of an intuitive, modern and user friendly interface is to provide the user a
learning experience regarding design process and theory of wind turbine design. Real time 3-D
visualization enables a user to visualize real time simulation environment as well as to study
different parts of wind turbine. Model templates make it possible for a new user to customize a
wind turbine design without starting from scratch. Advanced integrated analyses enable a full scale
simulation of a wind turbine when subjected to wind loads, sea waves, gravity, buoyancy, and
generator loads. ASHES is capable of doing simultaneous analyses and thus reduces the total
duration of simulation time. Therefore, efficient parallel jobs can be performed in ASHES. [2]
2.1.2   Technical specifications of ASHES  
ASHES combines a co-rotational formulation of beam elements with the finite element method
(FEM) in order to calculate the dynamic response of the wind turbine structure. The main reason to
use co-rotational formulation is to find numerical solutions as correct as possible in arbitrarily large
displacements and rotations in space. Either Newton-Raphson iterative scheme or linear solution of
the equations of motion is used depending on the importance of nonlinear effects. Model, analysis
type and characteristics of the load cases play important role in order to decide which type of solver
should be used in ASHES. [2] 
              ASHES calculates aerodynamic loads and wave loads using the Blade Element Momentum
(BEM) method and the Morison equation respectively. Both of them are coupled to the wind turbine
structural response. ASHES also supports different models such as tower shadow, hub loss, tip loss,
steady wind, yawed inflow, wind shear, different turbulence models, linear and nonlinear buoyancy
loads. [2]
             ASHES has a proportional-integral (PI) control system which can be customized to control
the generator torque and pitch angle of the wind turbine blades. It also supports external dynamic
link library (DLL) file compatible with Bladed as a control system. [2]
         ASHES utilizes Qt framework and OpenGL for the user interface and 3-D visualization
respectively. Object-oriented principles of C++ are used to write all the code of ASHES. ASHES is
currently available on Windows desktops. [2]
15
==PAGE:18==
2.1.3   Input files of ASHES  
To run a wind turbine simulation, ASHES requires input files for Blades and Airfoils. The files are
stored as text files so that the user can easily modify the files according to the requirements.
Existing blades and airfoils are stored in the folder: C:\Users\<user>\Documents\Ashes\Data\Blades
and C:\Users\<user>\Documents\Ashes\Data\Foils respectively. [1]
         Blades folder contains subfolders for different wind turbine blades with unique input files.
Each subfolder consists of two or three text files in order to define a particular blade. The main file
and the shape file are mandatory while the structural file is the optional one. The purpose of the
main file is to include the name and relative path of the blade shape file and the blade structural file
(if structural file exists). The shape file is used to define the shape date required to calculate the
flow loads for a particular blade. This file is divided into four sets of data defined by a particular
keyword. The keywords are known as ‘File-Format’, ‘Foils’, ‘CircElts’ and ‘FoilElts’ respectively.
‘File-Format’ determines how the rest of the data of the file will be organized. Currently ASHES
format is available. The keyword ‘Foils’ is used to specify the airfoils used for a particular blade.
The data sets must contain an identification number and foil name in order to be used in the later
part of the shape file. ‘CircElts’ defines the circular elements at the root section of the blade. The
‘CircElts’ data sets consist of the number of the circular element, the twist angle in degrees, the total
length  and diameter of the circular element in meters, the drag coefficient and pitch axis distance.
The ‘FoilElts’ data sets specify the airfoils to be used after the circular elements of the blade. The
data lines contain the number of the element following the last number of the circular element, the
twist angle in degrees, the total length and chord length of the foil element in meters, the foil
number corresponding to the ‘Foils’ data sets and pitch axis distance. The blade structural file is an
optional file required only when the elastic analysis, controller functionality and self-start of the
turbine are of interest. The file contains the structural data of the wind turbine blade including blade
structural twist, mass density, flapwise stiffness, edgewise stiffness, torsional stiffness etc. [1]
       Airfoils folder contain two subfolders known as ‘coefficients’ and ‘Geometries’. All the
information regarding coefficients and geometries of the airfoils are stored as text files in these sub
folders. Coefficient file usually contain information regarding Reynolds number, the angle of attack
(typically from -180 degree to 180 degree), the lift coefficient, the drag coefficient and the moment
coefficient. [1]
         Geometry file defines the horizontal and vertical coordinates of the airfoil as a function of
chord length. This file determines the airfoil shape. Example of ASHES input files can be found in
Appendix.
16
==PAGE:19==
2.1.4   Output files and post processing tools of ASHES  
ASHES contains default ‘Sensors’ window which displays the instantaneous value of different
sensors placed on different parts of the wind turbine. Each sensor is coupled to a corresponding
graph which can be visualized through ‘Graphs’ window. [1]     
             Figure 2.2 illustrates an example of sensors window and corresponding graph of ASHES.
Figure 2.2 (a) Sensors window and (b) corresponding graph of ASHES [1]
         The time-series value of a corresponding sensor can be viewed in a table. The table can also
be exported as a text file. Excel or LibreOffice can be used as a post processing tool to analyze the
text file. [1]   
2.2   HAWC2
HAWC2 stands for Horizontal Axis Wind turbine simulation Code 2nd generation. HAWC2 is an
aeroelastic code which performs the time domain simulation of the wind turbine response. The code
has been developed by the aeroelastic design research program at DTU Risø campus in Denmark.
[3]
2.2.1   Features of HAWC2
HAWC2 is capable of doing normal onshore wind turbine simulation with one or multiple blades.
The code is not only limited to Horizontal Axis Wind Turbine (HAWT) design rather Vertical Axis
Wind Turbines (VAWT) can also be designed and analyzed through HAWC2. HAWC2 can also
17
==PAGE:20==
study the response of pitch and active stall controlled wind turbines in time domain. HAWC2
supports a soil module which is mainly based on distribution of spring-damper force system.
Moreover, it also provides some exclusive features including guyed support structures, offshore
turbines on monopoles, tripods or jackets, floating turbines with mooring lines, multiple rotors in
one simulation, eigenvalue analysis at standstill, pitch regulated variable speed controller etc. [3]
             Figure 2.3 shows an illustration of HAWC2 features.
Figure 2.3 Illustration of HAWC2 features [4]
2.2.2   Technical specifications of HAWC2
HAWC2 follows  multi-body dynamics  for structural  modeling  which  is  capable  of handling
multiple degrees of freedom including blade torsion. Multi-body formulation is a general coupling
method for different independent structural bodies. The bodies can be either rigid or flexible. This
method is also known as ‘‘floating frame of reference’’. [5]
      In short, multi-body formulation combines different independent bodies using algebraic
constraints. The body joints can be either a fixed relative position among bodies or a bearing. Thus
a body is a summation of Timoshenko beam elements with six degrees of freedom in order to
account for stiffness, mass and inertia. [5]
           The aerodynamic part of HAWC2 is mainly based on the blade element momentum method.
But the aerodynamic model is different from classic one so that it can cope with dynamic inflow,
skew inflow, dynamic stall models including Stig Øye model, modified Beddoes-Leishmann model,
ATEF (Active Trailing Edge Flaps) model, yawed operation etc. Wind shear effects on induction,
Prandtl tip loss factor, tower drag and tower shadow effects are also included in the detailed
aerodynamic model of HAWC2. [3]
18
==PAGE:21==
         The hydrodynamic model of HAWC2 follows Morrison equation in order to determine the
impact of wave loading. The model covers an extensive range of water kinematics such as currents,
linear airy waves, irregular airy waves, deterministic irregular waves and stream function waves. [3]
        Wind, turbulence and wake modeling are also possible with HAWC2. It provides build-in
Mann turbulence generator for fully coherent 3D-turbulence and dynamic wake meandering model
for wind farm simulation. HAWC2 is also capable of reading Veers turbulence model files which
are mainly used in FLEX5. [3]
            Figure 2.4 illustrates the basis of multi-body formulation.
Figure 2.4 Multi-body formulation of HAWC2 [4]
2.2.3   Input files of HAWC2  
The input files of HAWC2 can be categorized as: main input file, beam cross-sectional data file,
aerodynamic layout blade file and aerodynamic profile coefficients file. [6] Example of HAWC2
input files can be found in Appendix.       
        Main Input file of HAWC2 is basically a text file known as the ‘htc’ file which represents the 
19
==PAGE:22==
Figure 2.5 Main command blocks of the ‘htc’ file [6]
global     definition of the structure such as location of the nodes, number of bodies, orientation of
the  coordinate  systems  etc.  The  ‘simulation’ block  is  used  to  define  the  global  simulation
parameters such as time duration of simulation, convergence limit etc. In the ‘new_htc_structure’
block, the entire structure of the wind turbine is defined. The ‘main_body’ code block is used to
configure each body and is repeated depending on the number of the main bodies. The ‘orientation’
code block is used to specify the desired orientations of the main bodies. The constraints among the
different main bodies are specified in the ‘constraint’ code block. The ‘wind’ block is used to
specify the wind climate such as wind density, wind speed, turbulence intensity, shear format etc.
The ‘hydro’ block is used to configure parameters related to hydrodynamics. The ‘dll’ block is used
to introduce controllers, external forces etc. Another important command block is the ‘output’
block. The main purpose of this block is to save the result of the dynamic behavior of the wind
20
==PAGE:23==
turbine as a data file. [6]
             The main command blocks of the ‘htc’ file are shown in Figure 2.5.
          Beam cross-sectional data file or ‘st’ file is used to define cross-sectional properties for the
Timoshenko beams such as mass, inertia, stiffness, modulus of elasticity, modulus of rigidity, shear
factor, area, structural twist, shear and elastic centers etc. [6]
      Usually 19 key structural input parameters are required for a particular ‘st’ file. These
parameters are shown in Table 2.1.

         Aerodynamic layout blade file or ‘ae’ file of HAWC2 is used to define the aerodynamic layout

such as lift, drag and moment coefficients as a function of angle of attack. [6]

2.2.4   Output files and post processing tools of HAWC2
HAWC2 can produce different types of output. The output can be classified as: animation output,
time simulation output, log file output and eigen frequency output. A wide range of post processing
tools are also available in order to analyze different types of output. [7]  
       The animation output can be used to check the orientation of different bodies. The animation
program is needed to visualize a HAWC2 animation output file. [7]  
        The time simulation output file is the most important output of HAWC2. The file represents the
time series results of various output sensors. Additional post processing tools such as Matlab and
Python scripts are required to analyze the output data. A GUI program known as pydap can also be
used to analyze and plot the output data. [7]  
         The log file output is useful in order to verify whether a simulation is carried out without an
error or not. Notepad++ program is required to analyze this log file output. [7]  
        The eigen frequency output determines the eigenmodes of the eigenfrequency analysis. The
rudimentary visualization program, known as animation program, can be used to visualize the
eigenmodes. [7]  
2.3   Basic definitions of Wind Turbine
To learn the details regarding Blade Element Momentum (BEM) method, one has to familiarize
with  some  basic  definitions  of  wind  turbine  including  angle  of  attack,  lift  coefficient,  drag
coefficient, axial induction factor, tangential induction factor etc.
Figure 2.6 Definition of lift and drag [8]
        Figure 2.6 represents an overview of angle of attack, lift and drag of a 2-D aerofoil. An
aerofoil is the base unit of a wind turbine blade. The front end and back end of aerofoil are known
22
==PAGE:25==
as leading edge and trailing edge respectively. The line connecting leading edge and trailing edge is
defined as chord line. 
        Angle of attack is defined as the angle between the chord line, c and the velocity at infinity, V∞
(or relative velocity) of aerofoil. It is usually expressed by α. [8] 
        Lift force can be defined as the force that acts perpendicular to the velocity at infinity, V∞ and is
responsible to overcome the gravity. Lift force is symbolized as L. 
        On the other hand, drag force is the force acting parallel to the velocity at infinity, V∞. Drag
force, D has to be balanced against a propulsion force of an engine in order to maintain a constant
speed. Reacting force, F is the resultant force of lift and drag force. [8] 
Now, lift coefficient and drag coefficient can be defined as follows
and
Here, ρ is the density and c is the chord length. [8]
        The main purpose of a wind turbine is to extract mechanical energy from wind energy and
transform it to electrical energy with the help of a generator. Now, if an ideal permeable rotor disc is
considered, it will work to slow down the wind speed far upstream of the rotor (V0) gradually at the
rotor plane (u) and also in the far wake or downstream of the rotor (ul). [8]
Figure 2.7 Illustration of the streamlines past the rotor and the axial velocity and pressure up and
downstream of the rotor [8]

2.6 Glauert Correction
The momentum theory is not valid for higher values of axial induction factor. Different states of the
rotor can be attributed for this failure of momentum theory. Therefore a correction (Glauert
correction) has to be applied. Depending on the critical Glauert value (either 1/3 or 0.2), Glauert
correction can be of two types: Glauert empirical formula and Wilson and Walker method. Figure
2.11 illustrates a comparative picture of Glauert correction (two types) against the momentum
theory. [8] 
Figure 2.11 Glauert correction (empirical formula and Wilson and Walker method) versus
momentum theory [8]
The following correction of thrust coefficient in terms of axial induction factor has to be enforced:
[8]

2.7 BEM formulation for HAWC2
HAWC2 adopts a slightly different BEM formulation than the one described in the earlier section.
Classical BEM approach considers uniform thrust and induction over each annular blade element
whereas in HAWC2 the overall rotor area is divided into a non-rotating polar grid (see Figure 2.12).
As a result, the induced velocities can be calculated at every point on the grid for each annulus
section. Moreover, it enables HAWC2 to realize the effects of radial and azimuthal variations of the
induction simultaneously. Hence, HAWC2 suits better in case of non-uniform inflow operation such
as sheared inflow or turbulence. [9]
Figure 2.12 HAWC2 polar grid for induction [9]
        Another major difference is that BEM considers thrust coefficient as a function of axial
induction factor (a) and Glauert correction should be applied for higher values of a. But HAWC2
considers the opposite relationship between thrust coefficient and axial induction factor (see Figure
2.13). Hence, axial induction factor is solved for a third order polynomial function of thrust
coefficient. The polynomial function contains the Glauert correction. [9]

Mathematically, axial induction factor of HAWC2 can be expressed as [9]
        BEM in HAWC2 also suffers from the same limitations of classical BEM. One of the major
limitations is that it cannot determine the effect of aerodynamic loads variation of adjacent radial
positions. Moreover, the same relation between thrust coefficient and axial induction factor has no
validation in case of curved rotors. Therefore, the values of the coefficients should be calculated
using alternative approach such as CFD (Computational Fluid Dynamics) simulations. [9]
2.8 Prandtl’s Tip Loss Factor in HAWC2
Prandtl’s tip loss factor is also applied in HAWC2 in order to correct the effect of vortex structure in
the wake created by a rotor with finite number of blades. The same type of correction factor (F) is
used for HAWC2 based on Wilson and Lissaman modification. Moreover, Prandtl’s tip loss factor
can be directly included in the third order polynomial function by introducing a modified thrust
coefficient (CT
*). [9]
Mathematically, Prandtl’s Tip Loss correction model for HAWC2:

2.9 Generator-Torque Controller for NREL 5-MW Baseline Wind Turbine
NREL 5-MW Baseline Wind Turbine requires a generator-torque controller which is based on the
fact that the generator torque is a function of the filtered generator speed. The controller needs to
function at five different control regions known as 1, 1½, 2, 2½ and 3. [10]
        Figure 2.14 illustrates the different control regions of a baseline generator-torque controller.
Region 1 is the zero torque region where no power is extracted from the wind rather it is used for
the rotor start up. Region 2 can be defined as the optimizing power capture control region where a
constant or optimal tip-speed ratio should be maintained. In this region, generator torque is directly
proportional to the square of the generator speed. Region 3 is the constant power region. As a result,
Figure 2.14 Torque-versus-speed response of the variable-speed controller [10]

generator torque maintains an inversely proportional relationship with the filtered generator speed.
Region 1½, a linear transition region between Regions 1 and 2, works to limit the turbine's
operational range by enforcing a lower limit on the generator speed. Another linear transition region
is Region 2½ (between Regions 2 and 3). The purpose of this region is to limit tip speed and noise
emissions at rated power. [10]

Chapter 3
Case 1: DTU 10MW Reference Wind Turbine (Stiff Rotor)     
3.1   Description  
DTU-10MW Reference Wind Turbine (DTU 10MW RWT) is designed as a part of the Light Rotor
cooperation project between DTU Wind Energy and Vestas. The main focus is to optimize the
design as well as to increase the structural property and overall performances by taking into account
both aerodynamic, aero-servo-elastic and structural considerations. [11]
3.2   Procedure 
The whole procedure of DTU 10MW Reference Wind Turbine simulations are divided into the
following segments: 
3.2.1   Upscaling
DTU 10MW RWT is designed mainly by upscaling the NREL 5MW reference wind turbine by
applying the classical similarity rules based on the assumptions of geometrical similarity [11]. The
main scaling parameters are documented in Table 1.
Table 3.1 Scaling Parameters for DTU 10MW RWT [11]
Symbol
Scaling Formula
Description

==PAGE:37==
where, sf stands for geometric scaling factor obtained by:
           Therefore, the overall key parameters are achieved for DTU 10MW RWT. The parameters
are documented in Table 3.2.
3.2.2   Airfoil Selection and Rotor Layout
FFA-W3-xxx airfoils are used to make the layout of DTU 10MW RWT. The minimum relative
thickness is chosen as 24.1% in order to increase the stiffness and match the aerodynamics. The
thickest possible airfoil available in FFA-W3 airfoil series is of 36.0% relative thickness. Therefore,
two more airfoils with relative thickness of 48.0% and 60.0% are used between 36.0% thickness
and the cylinder part of the rotor. The first one (48.0%) is created by simple multiplication of the
coordinates normal to the chord of the 36.0% thick airfoil. The 60.0% airfoil section is created by
interpolation between the 48.0% airfoil and the cylinder part of the rotor.  Hence, five airfoil
sections (Figure 3.1) in FFA-W3 airfoil series of different relative thicknesses (24.1%, 30.1%,
36.0%, 48.0% and 60.0%) are used to construct the DTU 10MW RWT rotor. [11]
Figure 3.1 FFA-W3-xxx airfoil series with airfoils of relative thickness of 24.1%, 30.1%, 36.0%,
48.0%, and 60.0%

3.3   Simulation Setup    
For HAWC2 simulation, with the help of ‘htc master’ file, 22 ‘htc’ files are generated (from 4m/s to
25m/s). Also, batch file is created to run the simulations. Now, by double clicking the batch file, 22
simulations are performed in HAWC2. At the end of the HAWC2 simulations, result files are
created. Afterwards, using the post processing tool (MATLAB scripts), the corresponding results of
power, thrust, power coefficient and thrust coefficient are recorded (Table 3.4).
        For ASHES simulation, the corresponding blade, shape and structure files are created in
accordance to the HAWC2 ‘htc’ and data files. The geometry and coefficient files of the FFA-W3-
xxx airfoil sections are also created. Therefore, Ashes simulations for different wind speeds (from 4
to 25 m/s) are performed and corresponding results of power, thrust, power coefficient and thrust
coefficient are recorded (Table 3.5).

3.4   Result Analysis    
All of the simulations are performed for 450s but to remove the transient state, the values are
recorded after 150s. Therefore, the results are plotted by taking the average of the simulated results
for the recorded time period of 300s. The results of power, thrust, power coefficient and thrust
coefficient as function of wind speed for HAWC2 and ASHES simulations are plotted in Figure 3.2
to 3.5 respectively.
           From Figure 3.2, it can be clearly seen that the mechanical power from ASHES and HAWC2
simulations are quite similar up to the rated condition (11.4 m/s). Afterwards, as the wind speed
increases, power from ASHES starts to increase slightly compared to the HAWC2 simulation. An
important conclusion can be drawn that in order to achieve the rated power after the rated wind
38
==PAGE:41==
speed, the operating pitch angle for ASHES should be increased corresponding to the same pitch
angle used for HAWC2 simulation at the rated RPM. The required pitch angles for ASHES are
documented in Table 3.6.
        Figure 3.3 and 3.5 shows almost the same behavior of thrust force and thrust coefficient
respectively for HAWC2 and ASHES simulations for different wind speeds.
           The power coefficient also follows the same pattern for HAWC2 and ASHES simulations as
it can be seen from Figure 3.4.

3.5   Comparison of Code-to-Code Benchmarking between HAWC2 and ASHES
Figure 3.6 to 3.9 shows the mismatch of different properties (power, thrust, power coefficient and
thrust coefficient) between HAWC2 and ASHES simulations.
           Bulk of the power mismatch occurs before the rated wind speed for HAWC2 compared to
ASHES but diminishes eventually afterwards. Thus, power results in an overall mismatch of
-3.21%. On the other hand, HAWC2 matches thrust force from ASHES so perfectly that the overall
mismatch is within the range of 1%.
           HAWC2 underestimates the power coefficient and thrust coefficient by around 5.15% and
2.90% respectively compared to ASHES simulation.

3.6   Span Wise Distribution of Property in HAWC2 and ASHES
To show the span wise distribution of various properties like angle of attack, lift coefficient, drag
coefficient, axial induction factor, tangential induction factor, thrust loading and tangential loading,
some test cases of wind speeds (4 m/s, 6 m/s, 8 m/s, 10 m/s and 11 m/s) are chosen and simulations
are performed thereby.   
         The corresponding property distributions for 4 m/s, 6 m/s, 8 m/s, 10 m/s and 11 m/s are
plotted in Figure 3.10 to 3.44.
        Span wise distribution of angle of attack reveals that for different wind speeds, HAWC2
closely matches with ASHES.
         It is clearly seen from the span wise distribution of lift coefficient that ASHES follows the
same behavior like HAWC2 but with a larger offset. The noticeable difference is that HAWC2
reaches the maximum value of lift coefficient at 1.5 for different wind speeds whereas ASHES
reaches the peak at 3. This anomaly occurs due to the presence of a bug in ASHES which will be
explained later. In short, the span wise distribution of lift coefficient in ASHES needs a correction
factor of 0.5 to match with HAWC2.
             In case of drag coefficient distribution, the output from HAWC2 resembles closely with that
from ASHES for different wind speeds. Drag coefficient tends to become almost zero for most parts
of the blade radius which is the expected behavior.   
        Span wise distribution of axial induction factor reveals interesting phenomena. ASHES
displays the same type of behavior as HAWC2 for most parts of the blade radius. However,
HAWC2 shows slight deviation from ASHES near the tip section. This type of difference is
expected as HAWC2 does not use BEM (Blade Element Momentum method) to calculate the
induced wind speeds. For both set of simulations, Prandtl's tip loss correction has been applied.
         In case of tangential induction factor distribution, the outputs from HAWC2 and ASHES
almost perfectly match with each other for different wind speeds.
            A slightly deeper insight into HAWC2 and ASHES requires the plotting of span wise
distribution  of  thrust  loading  and  tangential  loading  for  the  above  mentioned  wind  speeds.
Therefore, almost identical patterns are found for both HAWC2 and ASHES.

Chapter 4
Case 2: NREL 5-MW Baseline Wind Turbine (Flexible Rotor)
    
4.1   Description  
NREL 5-MW Baseline Wind Turbine is used extensively in the assessment of offshore wind power
technology throughout the world. The turbine has been developed by the U.S. Department of
Energy’s (DOE’s) National Renewable Energy Laboratory (NREL). [10] The main focus is to
develop a standardized design based on aerodynamics, hydrodynamics, wave dynamics and control
systems so that it can serve as a guideline for the state of the art offshore HAWT system and its
aeroelastic code development. [12]
4.2   Procedure
The whole procedure of NREL 5-MW Baseline Wind Turbine simulations are divided into the 
following segments:
4.2.1   Key Design Parameters of the NREL 5-MW Baseline Wind Turbine
To establish the basic design parameters of the NREL 5-MW Baseline Wind Turbine, combination
of publicly available wind turbine prototypes and conceptual models were taken into consideration.
After detailed assessment, it has been found out that the conceptual model described in the Dutch
Offshore Wind Energy Converter (DOWEC) project matched better with the properties of the
available wind turbine prototype known as REpower 5M. Therefore, the NREL 5-MW Baseline
Wind Turbine mainly contains the maximum data from the DOWEC study and the rest is based on
the properties of the REpower 5M. [10]
           Table 4.1 describes all the key design parameters of NREL 5-MW Baseline Wind Turbine.
64

To select the airfoils for the NREL 5-MW Baseline Wind Turbine, DOWEC study [10] has been
followed. Table 4.2 represents the airfoil data tables for the NREL 5-MW Baseline Wind Turbine.

of relative thickness are used to construct the first two-thirds of the blade span. The final one-third
of the blade span consists of NACA 64-series airfoils. [12]
           Apart from the airfoils, two cylinders with drag coefficients of 0.5 and 0.35 respectively are
used at the innermost section of the blade radius [10]. Figure 4.1 illustrates the airfoils used to make
the layout of the NREL 5-MW Baseline Wind Turbine.

4.2.3   Controller Properties
The comparison has been accounted for flexible rotor condition of the NREL 5-MW Baseline Wind
Turbine. As a result, the control system has to be activated and tuned perfectly. Therefore, some key
baseline properties are maintained for the control system [10]. The properties are documented in

4.3   Simulation Setup
For HAWC2 simulation, with the help of the above mentioned procedure section, 23 ‘htc’ files are
generated (from 3 m/s to 25 m/s). Data files including ‘st’ file, ‘ae’ file and ‘pc’ file are also created.
A batch file is created to run the simulations. Now, by double clicking the batch file, 23 simulations
67
==PAGE:70==
are performed in HAWC2. At the end of the HAWC2 simulations, result files or output files are
created. Afterwards, using the post processing tool (MATLAB scripts), the corresponding results of
power, thrust, power coefficient and thrust coefficient are recorded (Table 4.4).

         From Figure 4.2, it is obvious that the mechanical power from ASHES coincided with
HAWC2 for different wind speeds. Although the turbine reaches the rated power slightly earlier in
case of ASHES, the control system of HAWC2 reduces the overshoot such that the wind turbine
operates in the rated condition.  
          In the previous chapter, it has been discussed that the pitch angle in ASHES should be
increased in order to produce the same amount of power as HAWC2. The same scenario can be seen
from the flexible rotor simulation case. Only exception is that the control system determines the
pitch angle automatically for ASHES and HAWC2 whereas the stiff rotor case required manual
70

speeds are documented in Table 4.6.

speeds in case of ASHES and HAWC2.

4.5   Comparison of Code-to-Code Benchmarking between HAWC2 and ASHES
Figure 4.8 to 4.11 shows the mismatch of different properties (power, thrust, power coefficient and
thrust coefficient) between HAWC2 and ASHES simulations.
           Power mismatch follows a decreasing pattern until the rated wind speed. Afterwards, the
mismatch eventually flattens out towards 0%. This results in an overall power mismatch of -2.53%.
On the other hand, the overall thrust force mismatch is within the range of 0.6%. Bulk of it occurs
before the rated wind speed.   
           HAWC2 underestimates the power coefficient and thrust coefficient by around 5.2% and
2.55% respectively compared to ASHES simulation. Bulk of the mismatches happen before the
rated condition (11.4 m/s).   
Figure 4.8 Comparison of power from HAWC2 simulation with ASHES for the flexible rotor

flexible rotor

4.6   Span Wise Distribution of Property in HAWC2 and ASHES
Some test cases of wind speeds (4 m/s, 6 m/s, 8 m/s, 10 m/s and 11 m/s) are chosen in order to show
the span wise distribution of various properties like angle of attack, lift coefficient, drag coefficient,
axial  induction  factor,  tangential  induction  factor,  thrust loading  and  tangential  loading.  The
simulations are performed and results are recorded thereby. Figure 4.12 to 4.46 illustrate the span
wise property distributions for 4 m/s, 6 m/s, 8 m/s, 10 m/s and 11 m/s respectively.
          Span wise distribution of angle of attack reveals an identical match for both set of ASHES
and HAWC2 simulations. Angle of attack is usually higher in the root section and eventually
flattens out towards 0 degree near the blade tip.   
           In case of span wise distribution of lift coefficient, a significant amount of ASHES deviation
is clearly visible compared to HAWC2. ASHES follows the same behavior but deviates almost
twice as much as HAWC2. This anomaly occurs due to the presence of a bug in ASHES which was
also evident in the previous chapter. Hence, the span wise distribution of lift coefficient in ASHES
needs a correction factor of 0.5 in order to match with HAWC2.
           In case of drag coefficient distribution, the output from HAWC2 resembles closely with that
from ASHES for different wind speeds. Drag coefficient reduces drastically from a higher value
near the blade root section and remains almost zero for the rest of the blade span.
76
==PAGE:79==
          Span wise distribution of axial induction factor illustrates nothing but the identical behavior
for both set of ASHES and HAWC2 simulations. For both set of simulations, Prandtl’s tip loss
correction has been applied for both set of simulations.  
           In case of tangential induction factor distribution, the outputs from HAWC2 are coincided
with ASHES for different wind speeds. However, ASHES reaches the peak value near the blade root
section slightly earlier than HAWC2.   
       A slightly deeper insight into HAWC2 and ASHES requires the plotting of span wise
distribution of thrust loading and tangential loading for the above mentioned wind speeds. For both
case (thrust loading and tangential loading distribution), ASHES presides over HAWC2 with little
offset not as severe as in the case of lift coefficient distribution.

Chapter 5
Case 3: NTNU Blind Test (NTNU Stiff Rotor)
5.1 NTNU Blind Test
NTNU Blind Test (NTNU Stiff Rotor) was performed in the wind tunnel of Department of Energy
and Process Engineering at the Norwegian University of Science and Technology (NTNU) in a
view to predict data at a model scale using Computational Fluid Dynamics (CFD) method and
Blade Element Momentum (BEM) method. The rational comparison between two methods and
measured result was also done. [13]
        This is one of the recognized ways in order to calibrate the performance of aeroelastic software
like ASHES and HAWC2. Before doing so, the details regarding the model wind turbine including
description,  airfoil  coordinates,  aerodynamic  profile  layout,  chord  and  twist  definition,  and
operational conditions have to be specified.
5.2   Description
NTNU Stiff Rotor is a model wind turbine consists entirely of NREL S826 airfoil profile. The
diameter of the rotor is approximately 0.9 m. In order to reduce the gap in Reynolds number, the
chord length of the NTNU Stiff Rotor was made 3 times wider than a typical commercial wind
turbine. [13]
         The key design parameters of the NTNU Stiff Rotor are included in Table 5.1. The parameters
are obtained from the dimensions provided in Blind test 2 workshop (see Figure 5.1). This is
because the downstream turbine of Blind test 2 matches the turbine specifications of Blind test 1.
[14]
5.3   Airfoil Coordinates, Aerodynamic Profile Layout, Chord and Twist Definition
The NREL S826 airfoil profile is used to construct the entire span of the NTNU Stiff Rotor.
Previously, airfoil of the NACA 632 family was used for the model turbine but resulted in a poor
95
==PAGE:98==
peak power coefficient (CP around 0.28) at designed conditions. Hence, the NREL S826 airfoil
profile has been chosen for better performance and also for less Re -dependent design. [15]  
Figure 5.1 NTNU Stiff Rotor specifications used in Blind Test 1 [14]

5.4   Operating Conditions
The model turbine should maintain some basic operating conditions. First of all, it has to be
98
==PAGE:101==
operated for constant wind speed of 10 m/s. For all the measurements and simulations, the blade
pitch angle should be fixed to 0 degree. The model turbine should be operated at its design Tip
Speed Ratio (TSR) of 6. It is defined as the design condition because the turbine will get the peak
power coefficient only at that TSR (λ = 6). [14]

5.5   Simulation Setup
Unfortunately, the comparison of code-to-experiments level can only be conducted with ASHES. It
was not possible to draw the comparison using HAWC2 because of the unavailability of the model
turbine structural data. ASHES is capable of performing the simulations for stiff rotor in absence of
a structural input file. The same feature is not currently available with HAWC2.
         To run simulation in ASHES, necessary changes have been made based on Table 5.1, 5.2 and
5.3. The brief procedure has been described below:
•
A new blade file has been created using data from Table 5.3.  Here the main blade file (BT.txt)
and shape file (BT_Shape.txt) are created. As the blade is a stiff one, no structural file is needed.
•
A new  airfoil  geometry file  (NREL-S826-BT.txt)  has  been  created  using  Table  5.2.  The
corresponding coefficient file (NREL-S826-BT.txt) of the airfoil is same as the default airfoil
given as NREL-S826. The airfoil profile is shown in Figure 5.2.
•
After starting Ashes 2.1(Edu), a new project (Onshore 5MW) is selected.
•
Here only ‘RNA’ (Rotor Nacelle Assembly) option is used. ‘No Tower’ option is selected.
Hence, no hub height is required as in the case of NTNU Blind Test.
•
In Rotor properties, the newly created blade file (BT.txt) is selected.  
•
Hub mass is changed to 0.56780 kg instead of 56780 kg (since nothing is mentioned regarding
mass of the NTNU Blind Test rotor, a simple engineering guess is used).
•
Cone angle is changed to 0 (assumed from the inspection of Figure 5.1).
•
Hub radius is changed to 0.045 m (from the specification of NTNU Blind Test).
•
‘Full FE’ option is unchecked due to the fact that the rotor is a stiff one.
•
In Nacelle properties, both tilt angle and shaft height are changed to 0 (since nothing is found
from the specification). Nacelle mass is changed to 2.4 kg (reasonable engineering guess).
•
Main shaft ‘cross section’ is changed to pipe. Shaft radius and length are changed to 0.03 m and
0.1332 m respectively. Aft hor. distance is changed to 0.  Wall thickness is changed such that the
default ratio (0.2) between shaft radius and wall thickness remains the same.   
•
The wind flow type is ‘constant wind’ with 0 degree flow angle. Flow speed is always kept at 10
m/s.  
•
Hence, TSR (Tip Speed Ratio) is changed within a range starting from 1 to 11.3 with 0 degree
pitch angle and the corresponding CP and CT values are recorded (Table 5.5) in order to compare
them with the measured power and thrust coefficients from NTNU Blind Test (Table 5.4).
        All these changes are made to match the specification and test environment as close as
100
==PAGE:103==
possible. One possible way to verify the ASHES model with the NTNU Blind Test is to check the
tip Reynolds number at the design TSR (λ = 6). The measured tip Reynolds number from the
NTNU Blind Test was 1.0 × 105 [13]. Figure 5.4 provides a clear validation of Ashes model against
NTNU Blind Test.

5.6   Result Analysis
In the NTNU Blind Test, the maximum power coefficient (CP=0.448) was obtained at TSR=6. Same
thing happens in case of ASHES simulation (CP=0.448 at TSR, λ=6).
         Here, Figure 5.5 shows that Ashes slightly over predicts power coefficient at low Tip Speed
Ratio (TSR). But the over prediction becomes severe in the higher region of Tip Speed Ratio (TSR).
The under prediction of airfoil drag could be responsible for this severe mismatch [15]. In fact,
ASHES shows quite a good match with the measured value of CP until TSR=10 (see Figure 5.6). An
overall relative error of -15.84% occurs despite the fact that the trend of power coefficient curve is
almost same since the maximum power coefficient is achieved at TSR=6 for both case.
        In case of thrust coefficient, ASHES continuously under predicts in comparison to NTNU
Blind Test (Figure 5.7).  
         As ASHES follows BEM, one possible explanation could be finite domain size effect. In other
words, NTNU Blind Test was performed in a closed wind tunnel characterized by blockage effects.
But BEM does not consider blockage effects from the wind tunnel walls. Another possible reason
could be the under prediction of drag. [15]  
       From relative error curve (Figure 5.8), it can be said that as the TSR increases, the mismatch
decreases. It also gives an overall relative error of 15.59%.
       In case of ASHES model, a quantitative representation of power, thrust, torque and rotational
speed as a function of TSR is also shown in Table 5.6.

Chapter 6
Discussion     
6.1   Findings
The preliminary thesis objectives were to explore two aeroelastic wind turbine design software-
ASHES and HAWC2 in details and also to establish the comparison of benchmarking on code-to
code and code-to-experiments level. In order to do so, a total of three case studies were chosen.
         The first two cases dealt with the comparison of code-to code benchmarking. The cases were
distinguished between one another in terms of modeling and flexibility. The models are widely
renowned as DTU 10MW Reference Wind Turbine (Stiff Rotor) and NREL 5-MW Baseline Wind
Turbine (Flexible Rotor). A total of 45 different types of simulations were performed in both
ASHES and HAWC2. In doing so, it was found out that ASHES requires slightly higher operating
pitch angle compared to HAWC2 in order to produce the rated mechanical power at the rated RPM
(Table 6.1). Figure 6.1 and 6.2 also give clear indications about this important observation for the
above mentioned cases respectively. An overall pitch angle mismatch of 1.29% and 6.53% are
found for Case 1 and Case 2 respectively.

108
==PAGE:111==
         The last case was chosen to establish the comparison of code-to-experiments benchmarking.
Unfortunately,  the  comparison  of  code-to-experiments  level  was  conducted  only with  Ashes.
Absence of structural data input made it impossible to do so with HAWC2. A total of 22 different
types of simulations were also performed in ASHES in order to compare the analysis result from
NTNU Blind Test. In doing so, it was found out that ASHES severely over predicted the power
coefficient over higher regions of Tip Speed Ratio (TSR) in comparison with NTNU Blind Test.
The under prediction of airfoil drag was solely responsible for this mismatch. Moreover, in case of
thrust coefficient, ASHES illustrated a constant decremented behavior over the whole range of TSR.
The possible reasons were identified as under prediction of drag and blockage effects from the wind
tunnel walls. The effects are not covered by Blade Element Momentum (BEM) method and so do
by ASHES. 
6.2  Bugs in ASHES
As ASHES is still under development and is currently working on new improved features [1], it is
very normal to find bugs during the simulations. This thesis also contributed in a way to find out
some of the bugs that require corrections for future versions. Table 6.2 highlights some of the bugs
in ASHES faced and reported during the thesis period.

6.2.1  Bug in span wise ‘Cl’ or Lift Coefficient Sensor
To prove the point that the simulation of ASHES gives the non-identical distribution of lift
coefficient compared to HAWC2, Case 2 (NREL 5MW Flexible Rotor) was chosen and simulations
were done for 12 m/s both in ASHES and HAWC2. Afterwards, the following results of lift
coefficient distribution in ASHES and HAWC2 were plotted. From Figure 6.3 and 6.4, it can be
proven that ASHES and HAWC2 do not give the identical lift coefficient distribution despite having
the identical ‘Lift coefficients’ files in the data folders of ASHES and HAWC2. The amount of
deviation in ASHES is twice as much as HAWC2. The bug is reproducible and can also be shown
for Case 1 (see Chapter 2). 
Figure 6.3 Span wise distribution of lift coefficient in ASHES (wind speed = 12 m/s) 
Figure 6.4 Span wise distribution of lift coefficient in HAWC2 (wind speed = 12 m/s) 
110
==PAGE:113==
So in short, the non-identical distribution of lift coefficient in ASHES and HAWC2 occur because
of different calculation of Reynolds number mainly due to the presence of bug in ASHES.
6.2.2  Bug in the shape file of the Blades folder
In case of ASHES for Case 1, it was found out that if very small length (for example 4.44089×10-
16 m) of a ‘FoilElts’ or foil element was included in the shape file of the Blades folder of ASHES,
the software could not run. The bug is reproducible for different small lengths of foil element.
Figure 6.5 gives a fair indication of this anomaly.
Figure 6.5 Bug in the shape file of the Blades folder
Analyzing the figure, it can be said that ASHES does not accept such small length of foil element
which causes an error and ultimately stops the simulation.
6.2.3  Bug in ‘Flow speed’
In case of any simulation, ASHES starts with a default wind speed of 12 m/s even if the ‘Flow
speed’ parameter of ASHES is changed prior to start of simulation. Figure 6.6 indicates this
anomaly for Case 2. Here, the flow speed is indicated as 20 m/s, but the sensor 'Wind speed at hub'
indicates 12 m/s. 
           The wind speed can only be changed after the start of the simulation. The bug is reproducible
for every single simulation of ASHES.
111
==PAGE:114==
Figure 6.6 Bug in ‘Flow speed’
6.3  Bugs in HAWC2
Although HAWC2 has been developed and used widely for several years in the aeroelastic wind
turbine design purpose, it still has a lot of scopes for improvement in the future. First of all,
HAWC2 can not simulate a stiff rotor without the presence of structural data or ‘st’ file whereas
ASHES can (verified by Case 2). Moreover, the presence of bugs sometimes makes it difficult for
the user to predict the actual behavior of the model wind turbine. This thesis also deals with one
such bug during the implementation of HAWC2. Table 6.3 gives a short overview of HAWC2 bug
encountered during the thesis period.
Table 6.3 Bug reported in HAWC2

Fixing

Reported

reveals that it does not follow

6.3.1  Bug in ‘CT’ or thrust coefficient sensor
According to HAWC2 formulation, the axial induction factor is expressed as a function of thrust
coefficient. The function is a third order polynomial function which by default includes Glauert
correction. [9]
          But the simulation of Case 2 reveals a totally different phenomenon. Figure 6.7 proves that
the default ‘CT’ or thrust coefficient sensor of HAWC2 does not follow Glauert correction near the
tip section of the blade. The simulation is done for 12 m/s and comparison has been done by
computing thrust coefficient of HAWC2 and ASHES following Glauert Empirical Formula and
Wilson & Walker method respectively. It is clearly evident from the figure that near the blade tip
section (after 47.1 m) the deviation takes place for the default ‘CT’ or thrust coefficient sensor. The
bug is reproducible in nature and should have a high fixing priority.
Figure 6.7 Bug in ‘CT’ or thrust coefficient sensor
6.4  Proposed solution for Bugs in ASHES and HAWC2
It was kind of a challenge in order to deal with the bugs present in ASHES and HAWC2. The thesis
therefore tries to find alternative solutions in order to continue the comparison of code-to-code and
113
==PAGE:116==
code-to-experiments benchmarking.
6.4.1  ASHES solution
To deal with the prevailed bug in span wise ‘Cl’ or lift coefficient sensor of ASHES, the thesis
introduces a correction factor in the measurement of span wise lift coefficient distribution. As it is
obvious that the amount of anomaly is almost double than HAWC2, a correction factor of 0.5 has to
be multiplied with the current value of span wise ‘Cl’ or lift coefficient sensor in ASHES. In this
alternative way, the span wise lift coefficient distribution of different model turbines can be
predicted.
         Bug in the shape file of the Blades folder of ASHES can be handled by ignoring a particular
foil element of negligible lengths (for example 4.44089×10-16 m or similar). This type of method
was adapted in Case 1 and no significant deviations were found. Hence, the simulation in ASHES
can be continued without any interruption.
          At present, it is not possible to change the default flow speed before the start of the simulation
in ASHES. Hence, in order to compensate that the data should be recorded or taken into account
after a certain time period. Currently, the thesis considers this period as 150 seconds. In this way,
the bug and also the transient state can be avoided.
6.4.2  HAWC2 solution
Bug in ‘CT’ or thrust coefficient sensor of HAWC2 can be solved temporarily by using alternative
calculation of thrust coefficient. In order to do so, Glauert empirical formula should be used as
critical Glauert value is 0.33 for HAWC2. At first, the data of axial induction factor and tangential
induction factor should be recorded from default HAWC2 sensors. The ratio between the local
rotational speed and wind speed should be determined. Next, flow angle is calculated using axial
induction factor, tangential induction factor and the ratio between the local rotational speed and
wind speed. Prandtl’s tip loss correction factor is also calculated for a 3 bladed wind turbine. Last
but not the least, thrust coefficient should be calculated for HAWC2 using Glauert empirical
formula.
6.5  Features to be added in the future version
Apart from fixing the bugs, the future versions of ASHES and HAWC2 should also include some
114
==PAGE:117==
interesting features. First of all, ASHES should include the feature of simulating curved blades.
ASHES should also include the feature of span wise  ‘CT’ or thrust coefficient sensor. It is not
available in the current version. Therefore, Wilson and Walker method was adapted in order to do
so. Moreover, ASHES needs  to introduce a feature which will allow the user to record the
simulation results after a certain time period. ASHES should also introduce a feature which will
enable to predefine the duration of total simulation time. Currently it is not possible to stop the
simulation automatically.
         On the other hand, HAWC2 should introduce a feature of simulating stiff rotor without the
need of structural data or ‘st’ file like ASHES. Moreover, it will be a remarkable achievement if
HAWC2 can introduce a real time user friendly graphical interface like ASHES. Last but not the
least, HAWC2 should include features of exporting and importing the design of wind turbine
suitable for other software like ASHES, FAST, AutoCAD, SOLIDWORKS etc.
115
==PAGE:118==
Chapter 7
Conclusion
The overall performance of ASHES and HAWC2 reveals that without some notable exception (for
example spanwise lift coefficient distribution), two aeroelastic software show almost identical
behaviors in terms of comparison on code-to code and code-to-experiments level. Although there
will always be scope for improvement in the future version, a rational scrutiny of the current
version is inevitable. That was the main zest of the thesis in order to work as a basic guideline for
comparison of code-to-code and code-to-experiments benchmarking.
         For comparison of code-to-code benchmarking, DTU 10MW Reference Wind Turbine and
NREL 5-MW Baseline Wind Turbine were chosen for case study with stiff and flexible rotor
condition respectively. A brief overview of the two cases was performed afterwards. The analysis
includes basic property distribution like mechanical power, thrust, power coefficient and thrust
coefficient. A relative comparison of basic property distribution between ASHES and HAWC2 was
also presented. Moreover, spanwise property distribution of angle of attack, lift coefficient, drag
coefficient, axial induction factor, tangential induction factor, thrust loading and torque loading
were also plotted for ASHES and HAWC2.
         On the other hand, NTNU Blind Test (NTNU Stiff Rotor) was chosen as a case study for
comparison of code-to-experiments benchmarking. Due to the unavailability of structural data, the
comparison was limited to ASHES as HAWC2 is not capable of doing so without the presence of
structural input file or ‘st’ file. The analysis includes basic property variation against Tip Speed
Ratio (TSR) such as power coefficient and thrust coefficient. A relative error analysis between
ASHES and NTNU Blind Test was also performed and resulted in a relative error of -15.84% and
15.59% respectively. The possible reasons were also pointed out as under prediction of airfoil drag
and blockage effects from the wind tunnel walls.
         The overall result analysis reveals some important facts about ASHES and HAWC2. One of
the most important observations is the fact that ASHES requires slightly higher pitch angle in order
to attain the rated power at the rated rotational speed. The overall amount varies from 1.29% to
6.53% for the two cases respectively. Finding bugs and their fixing priority can be glorified as the
greatest achievement of the thesis. In doing so, the severe anomaly is found in case of spanwise ‘Cl’
or lift coefficient sensor of ASHES as it gives almost double value of lift coefficient in comparison
with HAWC2. Moreover, ASHES contain bugs in ‘Flow speed’ parameter and the shape file of the
116
==PAGE:119==
Blades folder. Although HAWC2 is a renowned aeroelastic code, it is also vulnerable to bugs such
as ‘CT’ or thrust coefficient sensor bug.
         The thesis also contributes in a way to find the possible solutions of the current problems.
ASHES bugs can be fixed by introducing a correction factor of 0.5 in lift coefficient calculation, by
ignoring any particular foil element of negligible lengths in the shape file and by sampling data file
to compensate for ‘Flow speed’ parameter bug. Meanwhile, HAWC2 bug can be fixed by using
alternative calculation (Glauert empirical formula) of thrust coefficient. Moreover, the thesis also
highlights the need of some interesting features to be added in the future versions of ASHES and
HAWC2.
         Every single thing has its pros and cons. One of the limitations that the thesis suffered from
was its incapability to replicate the NTNU Blind Test results in HAWC2. Also, it was not possible
to compare various analysis results and options like tip deflection, blade root moment, tower top
moment, tower base moment, tower shadow feature etc. due to time limitation.
     The possible future works can be identified as comparison of code-to-experiments level
benchmarking of NTNU Blind Test in HAWC2 as well as validation and verification of IEC Design
Load Cases (DLC) in ASHES and HAWC2 for the study cases discussed in the thesis. Therefore, it
will finally determine the endurance level and fatigue limit of DTU 10MW Reference Wind Turbine
and NREL 5-MW Baseline Wind Turbine.
         Apart from some limitations and setbacks, it can be concluded that the thesis serves as a basic
guideline to do various types of dynamic simulations in two aeroelastic software-ASHES and
HAWC2. Moreover, the thesis also performs as a review paper from a user perspective for the
current versions of ASHES and HAWC2 by exploring the bugs and their possible remedy. Hence,
ASHES as an aeroelastic software is not very far away to be used in the same bracket like HAWC2
in the near future.
117
==PAGE:120==

      body_eulerang 0.0 0.0 0.0;
    end base;
; 
    begin relative;
      body1  tower last;
      body2  towertop 1;
      body2_eulerang 0.0 0.0 0.0; 
    end relative;
;
    begin relative;
      body1  towertop last;
      body2  shaft 1;
      body2_eulerang 90.0 0.0 0.0; 
      body2_eulerang 5.0 0.0 0.0;    5 deg tilt angle
      body2_ini_rotvec_d1 0.0 0.0 -1.0 0.5 ; body initial rotation velocity x.y.z.angle velocity[rad/s]  (body 2 coordinates)
;      body2_ini_rotvec_d1 0.0 0.0 -1.0 0.9424 ; body initial rotation velocity x.y.z.angle velocity[rad/s]  (body 2 coordinates)
    end relative;
;
    begin relative;
      body1  shaft last;         
      body2  hub1 1;
      body2_eulerang -90.0 0.0 0.0;    
      body2_eulerang 0.0 180.0 0.0;    
      body2_eulerang 2.5 0.0 0.0;      2.5deg cone angle
    end relative;
;
==PAGE:209==
    begin relative;
      body1  shaft last;         
      body2  hub2 1;
      body2_eulerang -90.0 0.0 0.0;    
      body2_eulerang 0.0 60.0 0.0;   
      body2_eulerang 2.5 0.0 0.0;      2.5deg cone angle
    end relative;
;
    begin relative;
      body1  shaft last;         
      body2  hub3 1;
      body2_eulerang -90.0 0.0 0.0;    
      body2_eulerang 0.0 -60.0 0.0;    
      body2_eulerang 2.5 0.0 0.0;      2.5deg cone angle
    end relative;
;
    begin relative;
      body1  hub1 last;         
      body2  blade1 1;
      body2_eulerang 0.0 0.0 0;    
    end relative;
;
    begin relative;
      body1  hub2 last;         
      body2  blade2 1;
      body2_eulerang 0.0 0.0 0.0;    
    end relative;
;
    begin relative;
      body1  hub3 last;         
      body2  blade3 1;
      body2_eulerang 0.0 0.0 0.0;    
==PAGE:210==
    end relative;

  end orientation;
;-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------
begin constraint;   
;
    begin fix0;  fixed to ground in translation and rotation of node 1
      body tower;
    end fix0;
;
     begin fix1;
  body1 tower last ;
  body2 towertop 1;
end fix1;
;
    begin bearing1;                       free bearing
     name  shaft_rot;
      body1 towertop last;
      body2 shaft 1;
      bearing_vector 2 0.0 0.0 -1.0;        x=coo (0=global.1=body1.2=body2) vector in body2 coordinates where the free rotation is present
   end bearing1; 
;
     begin fix1;
  body1 shaft last ;
  body2 hub1 1;
end fix1;
;
     begin fix1;
  body1 shaft last ;
  body2 hub2 1;
end fix1;
;
==PAGE:211==
     begin fix1;
  body1 shaft last ;
  body2 hub3 1;
end fix1; 
; 
    begin bearing2;
      name pitch1; 
      body1 hub1 last;
     body2 blade1 1;
bearing_vector 2 0.0 0.0 -1.0;
   end bearing2;
;
    begin bearing2;
      name pitch2; 
      body1 hub2 last;
      body2 blade2 1;
bearing_vector 2 0.0 0.0 -1.0;
    end bearing2;
;
    begin bearing2;
      name pitch3; 
      body1 hub3 last;
      body2 blade3 1;
bearing_vector 2 0.0 0.0 -1.0;
    end bearing2;
end constraint;
;
end new_htc_structure;

begin wind ;
  density                 1.225 ; to be checked
  wsp                     4.0  ;
==PAGE:212==
  tint                    0.0 ;
  horizontal_input        1     ;            0=false, 1=true
  windfield_rotations     0.0  0.0  0.0 ;    yaw, tilt, rotation
  center_pos0             0.0 0.0 -90.0 ;
  shear_format            1  0.0  ;0=none,1=constant,2=log,3=power,4=linear
  turb_format             0     ;  0=none, 1=mann,2=flex
  tower_shadow_method     0     ;  0=none, 1=potential flow, 2=jet
;  scale_time_start        0 ;
;  wind_ramp_factor   0.0 [t0] [wsp factor] 1.0 ;
;  [gust] iec_gust [gust_type] [gust_A] [gust_phi0] [gust_t0] [gust_T] ;
end wind;
;
begin aero ;
  nblades  3;
  hub_vec shaft -3 ;         rotor rotation vector (normally shaft composant directed from pressure to sustion side)
  link 1 mbdy_c2_def blade1;
  link 2 mbdy_c2_def blade2;
  link 3 mbdy_c2_def blade3;
  ae_filename        ./data/NREL_5MW_ae.txt;
  pc_filename        ./data/NREL_5MW_pc.txt;
  induction_method   1 ;     0=none, 1=normal
  aerocalc_method    1 ;     0=ingen aerodynamic, 1=med aerodynamic
  aerosections       46 ;
  ae_sets            1 1 1;
  tiploss_method     1 ;     0=none, 1=prandtl
  dynstall_method    2 ;     0=none, 1=stig øye method,2=mhh method
end aero ;
;-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------  
begin dll;
  begin hawc_dll;
    filename  ./control/bladed2hawc.dll ;
    dll_subroutine regulation ;
==PAGE:213==
    arraysizes  15 15 ;
    deltat  0.02;    
    begin output;
      general time ;                                                                                                             1
      constraint bearing2 pitch1 1;  angle and angle velocity written to dll                                                     2,3
      constraint bearing2 pitch2 1;  angle and angle velocity written to dll                                                     4,5
      constraint bearing2 pitch3 1;  angle and angle velocity written to dll                                                     6,7
      constraint bearing2 shaft_rot 1; angle and angle velocity written to dll  (slow speed shaft)                               8,9
      wind free_wind 1 0.0 0.0 -90.0; local wind at fixed position: coo (1=global,2=non-rotation rotor coo.), pos x, pos y, pos z   10,11,12
      general constant 97.0 ;                  generator exchange ratio                                                              13
    end output;
;
    begin actions;    
      body moment_ext shaft 1 3;
    end actions;
  end hawc_dll;
;
  begin hawc_dll;
    filename  ./control/pitchservo_pos.dll ;
    dll_subroutine servo ;
    arraysizes  15 15 ;
    deltat    0.02 ;
    begin output;
      general time ;                                                                     1
      dll inpvec 1 2;                                                                    2
      dll inpvec 1 3;                                                                    3
      dll inpvec 1 4;                                                                    4
      constraint bearing2 pitch1 1;  angle and angle velocity written to dll         5,6
      constraint bearing2 pitch2 1;  angle and angle velocity written to dll         7,8
      constraint bearing2 pitch3 1;  angle and angle velocity written to dll         9,10
    end output;
;
==PAGE:214==
    begin actions;    
      body bearing_angle pitch1;
      body bearing_angle pitch2;
      body bearing_angle pitch3;
    end actions;
  end hawc_dll;
;
   begin hawc_dll;
    filename  ./control/damper.dll ;
    dll_subroutine damp ;
    arraysizes  15 15 ;
    begin output;
      general time ;                                                                     1
      general constant 5.0;
      general constant 10.0;
      general constant -1.0E1 ;
      mbdy state vel towertop 1 1.0 tower;
   end output;
;
    begin actions;    
       mbdy force_ext towertop 2 1 towertop;   
    end actions;
  end hawc_dll;
;
  begin type2_dll;
    name gear;
    filename  ./control/hss_convert.dll ;
    arraysizes_init  3 1 ;
    arraysizes_update  2 2 ;
    begin init;
      constant 1 1.0 ;     number of used sensors - in this case only 1
      constant 2 1000;     unit conversion factor
==PAGE:215==
    end init;
    begin output;
      mbdy momentvec shaft 1 1 shaft # only 3;
    end output;
;
    begin actions;    
      mbdy moment_ext towertop 2 3 shaft;
    end actions;
  end type2_dll;
;
end dll;
;

begin output;
  filename ./res/NREL_5MW_RWT_4 ;
time 150.0 450.0 ; 
  buffer 1 ;
  general time;
  ;data_format  hawc_binary;
;  
  constraint bearing1 shaft_rot 2; angle and angle velocity 
  constraint bearing2 pitch1 5;    angle and angle velocity 
  constraint bearing2 pitch2 5;    angle and angle velocity 
  constraint bearing2 pitch3 5;    angle and angle velocity 
  aero omega ;
  aero torque;
  aero power;
  aero thrust;
  wind free_wind 1 0.0 0.0 -90.0; local wind at fixed position: coo (1=global,2=non-rotation rotor coo.), pos x, pos y, pos z
;
  mbdy momentvec towertop 1 2 towertop # yaw bearing ;
==PAGE:216==
  mbdy forcevec  towertop 1 2 towertop # yaw bering ;
  mbdy momentvec shaft 4 1  shaft # main bearing ;
  mbdy momentvec blade1 3  1 blade1 # blade 1 root ;
  mbdy momentvec blade1 10 1 local # blade 1 50% local e coo ;
  mbdy momentvec hub1 1  2 hub1 # blade 1 root ;
  mbdy momentvec hub2 1  2 hub2 # blade 2 root ;
  mbdy momentvec hub3 1  2 hub3 # blade 3 root ;
  mbdy state pos towertop   1 1.0 global # tower top flange position ;
;
  mbdy state pos blade1  18 1.0 blade1 # blade 1 tip pos ;
  mbdy state pos blade2  18 1.0 blade2 # blade 2 tip pos ;
  mbdy state pos blade3  18 1.0 blade3 # blade 3 tip pos ;
  mbdy state pos blade1  18 1.0 global # blade 1 tip pos ;
  aero windspeed 3 1 1 63.0;   wind seen from the blade: coo(1=local ae,2=blade,3=global,4=rotor polar), blade number, component xyz, blade 
radius
  aero windspeed 3 1 2 63.0;  
  aero windspeed 3 1 3 63.0;
aero alfa 1 0.0000;
aero alfa 1 1.367;
aero alfa 1 4.100;
aero alfa 1 6.833;
aero alfa 1 10.250;
aero alfa 1 14.350;
aero alfa 1 18.450;
aero alfa 1 22.550;
aero alfa 1 26.650;
aero alfa 1 30.750;
aero alfa 1 34.850;
aero alfa 1 38.950;
aero alfa 1 43.050;
aero alfa 1 47.150;
aero alfa 1 51.250;
==PAGE:217==
aero alfa 1 54.667;
aero alfa 1 57.400;
aero alfa 1 60.133;
aero alfa 1 61.500;
  mbdy momentvec towertop 1 1 tower # tower top -1: below top mass ;
  mbdy forcevec  towertop 1 1 tower # tower top -1: below top mass ;
  mbdy momentvec tower  1 1 tower # tower base ;
  mbdy forcevec  tower  1 1 tower # tower base ;
;
  dll outvec 1 1 # time;
  dll outvec 1 2 # pitch angle 1;
  dll outvec 1 3 # pitch vel 1;
  dll outvec 1 4 # pitch angle 2;
  dll outvec 1 5 # pitch vel 2;
  dll outvec 1 6 # pitch angle 3;

  dll outvec 1 10 # free wind x;
  dll outvec 1 11 # free wind y;
  dll outvec 1 12 # free wind z;

  dll inpvec 1 1 # Mgen slow;

  dll outvec 2 1 # time;

  dll outvec 2 9 # pitch angle 3;
  dll outvec 2 10 # pitch speed 3;
end output;

for i=8
x=w(i).*r./V(i);
end
tanphi=(1-a)./((1+am).*x);
phi=atan(tanphi);
for i=8
Vrel=V(i).*(1-a)./sin(phi);
end
L=0.5*rho.*Vrel.^2.*c.*CL;%Lift force calculation for HAWC2
D=0.5*rho.*Vrel.^2.*c.*CD;%Drag force calculation for HAWC2
Pn=L.*cos(phi)+D.*sin(phi);%Thrust loading calculation for HAWC2
Pt=L.*sin(phi)-D.*cos(phi);%Tangential loading calculation for HAWC2

0.00543832 0.00477072 0.00446885 0.00437463 0.00420559];%tangential induction factor from ASHES sensor

Htanphi=(1-Ha)./((1+Ham).*Hx);

==PAGE:264==
Aphi=atan(Atanphi);%ASHES flow angle
B=3;%No. of blades
R=61.5;%Blade radius
HR1=R-Hr;
AR1=R-Ar;
Hf=exp((-B/2).*HR1./(Hr.*sin(Hphi)));
Af=exp((-B/2).*AR1./(Ar.*sin(Aphi)));
HF=2/3.1416.*acos(Hf);%HAWC2 Prandtl Tip loss correction factor
AF=2/3.1416.*acos(Af);%ASHES Prandtl Tip loss correction factor
HGac=0.33;%Critical Glauert correction value for HAWC2
Ha2=1-Ha;
Ha3=Ha2.*HF;
Ha4=5.-(3.*Ha);
HCt=zeros(18,1);
%For Glauert empirical relationship HAWC2 axial induction factor does not exceed critical value of 0.33
%only following rule is applicable to calculate CT
for i=1:18
    HCt=4.*Ha3(i).*Ha(i)
end
HGCT=[0.3794

0.5781
0.5487
0.5049
0.3434
0];%CT from Glauert empirical relation for HAWC2
%For Wilson & walker method, ASHES axial induction factor does exceed critical value of 0.2
%so the following rules are applicable to calculate CT
AWac=0.2;%Critical Glauert correction value for ASHES
Aa2=1-Aa;
Aa3=Aa2.*AF;
ACt=zeros(17,1);
for i=1:3
    ACt=4.*Aa3(i).*Aa(i)
end
for i=4:5
    ACt=4.*(AWac^2.+((1.-(2.*AWac)).*Aa(i))).*AF(i)
end
for i=6:14
    ACt=4.*Aa3(i).*Aa(i)
end
for i=15:17
    ACt=4.*(AWac^2.+((1.-(2.*AWac)).*Aa(i))).*AF(i)
end
AWCT=[0.3594

plot(Hr,CT,'-*',Hr,HGCT,'-*',Ar,AWCT,'-*')

DTU Vindenergi er et institut under Danmarks Tekniske Universitet med en unik integration af forskning, uddannelse, innovation og 
offentlige/private konsulentopgaver inden for vindenergi. Vores aktiviteter bidrager til nye muligheder og teknologier inden for udnyttelse af 
vindenergi, både globalt og nationalt. Forskningen har fokus på specifikke tekniske og videnskabelige områder, der er centrale for udvikling, 
innovation og brug af vindenergi, og som danner grundlaget for højt kvalificerede uddannelser på universitetet.  
 
Vi har mere end 240 ansatte og heraf er ca. 60 ph.d. studerende. Forskningen tager udgangspunkt i ni forskningsprogrammer, der er organiseret i 
tre hovedgrupper: vindenergisystemer, vindmølleteknologi og grundlagsskabende forskning.
